	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Peace Corps</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">US volunteer agency</div>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a href="/wiki/File:Peace_corps_logo16.svg" class="image"><img alt="Peace corps logo16.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Peace_corps_logo16.svg/250px-Peace_corps_logo16.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="127" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Peace_corps_logo16.svg/375px-Peace_corps_logo16.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Peace_corps_logo16.svg/500px-Peace_corps_logo16.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="152" data-file-height="77" /></a></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #efefef">Agency overview</th></tr><tr><th scope="row">Formed</th><td>March&#160;1, 1961<span class="noprint">&#59;&#32;58 years ago</span><span style="display:none">&#160;(<span class="bday dtstart published updated">1961-03-01</span>)</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Headquarters</th><td><a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, U.S.</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Annual budget</th><td>$398&#160;million<br />(2018 fiscal year)<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Agency executive</th><td><div class="plainlist"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Josephine_K._Olsen" class="mw-redirect" title="Josephine K. Olsen">Josephine K. Olsen</a>, Director</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Parent agency</th><td><a href="/wiki/US_Government" class="mw-redirect" title="US Government">US Government</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Website</th><td><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.peacecorps.gov">www<wbr />.peacecorps<wbr />.gov</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Peace Corps</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Volunteering" title="Volunteering">volunteer</a> program run by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">United States government</a>. Its official mission is to provide social and economic development abroad through technical assistance, while promoting mutual understanding between Americans and populations served. The program was established by <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Executive_Order_10924" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Executive Order 10924">Executive Order 10924</a>, issued by President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> on March 1, 1961 and authorized by <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a> on September 21, 1961 with passage of the Peace Corps Act (Pub.L. 87–293).
</p><p>Peace Corps Volunteers are American citizens, typically with a college degree, who work abroad for a period of two years after three months of training. Volunteers work with governments, schools, <a href="/wiki/Non-profit_organization" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-profit organization">non-profit organizations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-government_organization" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-government organization">non-government organizations</a>, and entrepreneurs in education, business, <a href="/wiki/Information_technology" title="Information technology">information technology</a>, agriculture, and the environment. After 24 months of service, volunteers can request an extension of service.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Since its inception, more than 235,000 Americans have joined the Peace Corps and served in 141 countries.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="1950.E2.80.931959"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1950–1959">1950–1959</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: 1950–1959">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_greeting_Peace_Corps_volunteers,_1961.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Kennedy_greeting_Peace_Corps_volunteers%2C_1961.jpg/220px-Kennedy_greeting_Peace_Corps_volunteers%2C_1961.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Kennedy_greeting_Peace_Corps_volunteers%2C_1961.jpg/330px-Kennedy_greeting_Peace_Corps_volunteers%2C_1961.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c9/Kennedy_greeting_Peace_Corps_volunteers%2C_1961.jpg/440px-Kennedy_greeting_Peace_Corps_volunteers%2C_1961.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="471" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kennedy_greeting_Peace_Corps_volunteers,_1961.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>John F. Kennedy greets volunteers on August 28, 1961</div></div></div>
<p>Following the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, various members of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> proposed bills to establish volunteer organizations in <a href="/wiki/Developing_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Developing countries">developing countries</a>. In December 1951 Representative <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> (D-<a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>) suggested to a group that "young college graduates would find a full life in bringing technical advice and assistance to the underprivileged and backward Middle East ... In that calling, these men would follow the constructive work done by the religious missionaries in these countries over the past 100 years."<sup id="cite_ref-leamer2001_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leamer2001-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>337–338</span></sup> In 1952 Senator <a href="/wiki/Brien_McMahon" title="Brien McMahon">Brien McMahon</a> (D-Connecticut) proposed an "army" of young Americans to act as "missionaries of democracy".<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Privately funded nonreligious organizations began sending volunteers overseas during the 1950s. While Kennedy is credited with the creation of the Peace Corps as president, the first initiative came from Senator <a href="/wiki/Hubert_H._Humphrey,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Hubert H. Humphrey, Jr.">Hubert H. Humphrey, Jr.</a> (D-<a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>), who introduced the first bill to create the Peace Corps in 1957—three years before Kennedy, as a presidential candidate, would raise the idea during a campaign speech at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Michigan" title="University of Michigan">University of Michigan</a>. In his autobiography <i>The Education of a Public Man</i>, Humphrey wrote,
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r886047036">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>There were three bills of particular emotional importance to me: the Peace Corps, a disarmament agency, and the Nuclear Test Ban Treaty. The President, knowing how I felt, asked me to introduce legislation for all three. I introduced the first Peace Corps bill in 1957. It did not meet with much enthusiasm. Some traditional diplomats quaked at the thought of thousands of young Americans scattered across their world. Many senators, including liberal ones, thought it silly and an unworkable idea. Now, with a young president urging its passage, it became possible and we pushed it rapidly through the Senate. It is fashionable now to suggest that Peace Corps Volunteers gained as much or more, from their experience as the countries they worked. That may be true, but it ought not demean their work. They touched many lives and made them better.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peace_Corps_headquarters.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Peace_Corps_headquarters.JPG/170px-Peace_Corps_headquarters.JPG" decoding="async" width="170" height="182" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Peace_Corps_headquarters.JPG/255px-Peace_Corps_headquarters.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Peace_Corps_headquarters.JPG/340px-Peace_Corps_headquarters.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2428" data-file-height="2606" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peace_Corps_headquarters.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Peace Corps headquarters at 1111 20th Street, NW in downtown Washington, D.C.</div></div></div>
<p>Only in 1959, however, did the idea receive serious attention in Washington when Congressman <a href="/wiki/Henry_S._Reuss" title="Henry S. Reuss">Henry S. Reuss</a> of <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> proposed a "Point Four Youth Corps". In 1960, he and Senator <a href="/wiki/Richard_L._Neuberger" title="Richard L. Neuberger">Richard L. Neuberger</a> of <a href="/wiki/Oregon" title="Oregon">Oregon</a> introduced identical measures calling for a nongovernmental study of the idea's "advisability and practicability". Both the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_Committee_on_Foreign_Affairs" title="United States House Committee on Foreign Affairs">House Foreign Affairs Committee</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate_Committee_on_Foreign_Relations" title="United States Senate Committee on Foreign Relations">Senate Foreign Relations Committee</a> endorsed the study, the latter writing the Reuss proposal into the pending <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Mutual_Cooperation_and_Security_between_the_United_States_and_Japan" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Mutual Cooperation and Security between the United States and Japan">Mutual Security legislation</a>. In this form it became law in June 1960. In August the Mutual Security Appropriations Act was enacted, making available US$10,000 for the study, and in November ICA contracted with <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Albertson" class="mw-redirect" title="Maurice Albertson">Maurice Albertson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_E._Rice" title="Andrew E. Rice">Andrew E. Rice</a>, and Pauline E. Birky of <a href="/wiki/Colorado_State_University" title="Colorado State University">Colorado State University</a> Research Foundation<sup id="cite_ref-m.rockymountainnews.com_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m.rockymountainnews.com-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> for the study.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1960.E2.80.931969"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1960–1969">1960–1969</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: 1960–1969">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>John F. Kennedy was the first to announce the idea for such an organization during the <a href="/wiki/U.S._presidential_election,_1960" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. presidential election, 1960">1960 presidential campaign</a>, on October 14, 1960, at a late-night speech at the University of Michigan in <a href="/wiki/Ann_Arbor,_Michigan" title="Ann Arbor, Michigan">Ann Arbor</a> on the steps of the <a href="/wiki/Michigan_Union" title="Michigan Union">Michigan Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> He later dubbed the proposed organization the "Peace Corps." A brass marker commemorates the place where Kennedy stood. In the weeks after the 1960 election, the study group at Colorado State University released their feasibility a few days before Kennedy's Presidential Inauguration in January 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Critics opposed the program. Kennedy's opponent, <a href="/wiki/Richard_M._Nixon" class="mw-redirect" title="Richard M. Nixon">Richard M. Nixon</a>, predicted it would become a "cult of escapism" and "a haven for <a href="/wiki/Draft_dodger" class="mw-redirect" title="Draft dodger">draft dodgers</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-founding_docs_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-founding_docs-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gibson_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gibson-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tobin_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tobin-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Others doubted whether recent graduates had the necessary skills and maturity for such a task. The idea was popular among students, however, and Kennedy pursued it, asking respected academics such as <a href="/wiki/Max_Millikan" title="Max Millikan">Max Millikan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chester_Bowles" title="Chester Bowles">Chester Bowles</a> to help him outline the organization and its goals. During his inaugural address, Kennedy again promised to create the program: "And so, my fellow Americans: ask not what your country can do for you—ask what you can do for your country".<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> President Kennedy in a speech at the White House on June 22, 1962, "Remarks to Student Volunteers Participating in Operation Crossroads Africa", acknowledged that <a href="/wiki/Operation_Crossroads_Africa" title="Operation Crossroads Africa">Operation Crossroads for Africa</a> was the basis for the development of the Peace Corps. "This group and this effort really were the progenitors of the Peace Corps and what this organization has been doing for a number of years led to the establishment of what I consider to be the most encouraging indication of the desire for service not only in this country but all around the world that we have seen in recent years".<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The Peace Corps website answered the question "Who Inspired the Creation of the Peace Corps?", acknowledging that the Peace Corps were based on Operation Crossroads Africa founded by <a href="/wiki/James_Herman_Robinson" title="James Herman Robinson">Rev. James H. Robinson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:102px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Executive_Order_10924_from_NARA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Physical text copy of the Executive Order establishing the Peace Corps" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Executive_Order_10924_from_NARA.jpg/100px-Executive_Order_10924_from_NARA.jpg" decoding="async" width="100" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Executive_Order_10924_from_NARA.jpg/150px-Executive_Order_10924_from_NARA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Executive_Order_10924_from_NARA.jpg/200px-Executive_Order_10924_from_NARA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="774" data-file-height="1260" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Executive_Order_10924_from_NARA.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Executive Order 10924</div></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg" title="File:JFK Establishment of the Peace Corps edit.ogg">Establishment of the Peace Corps</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="82.337959183673" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (71 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="71292" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/56/JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg/JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="135424" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0"><a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a>'s announcement of the establishment of the Peace Corps</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>On March 1, 1961, Kennedy signed <a href="/wiki/Executive_order_(United_States)" class="mw-redirect" title="Executive order (United States)">Executive Order</a> 10924 that officially started the Peace Corps. Concerned with the growing tide of revolutionary sentiment in the <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">Third World</a>, Kennedy saw the Peace Corps as a means of countering the <a href="/wiki/Stereotype" title="Stereotype">stereotype</a> of the "<a href="/wiki/Ugly_American_(pejorative)" title="Ugly American (pejorative)">Ugly American</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/American_imperialism" title="American imperialism">Yankee imperialism</a>," especially in the emerging nations of post-colonial Africa and Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Kennedy appointed his brother-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Sargent_Shriver" title="Sargent Shriver">Sargent Shriver</a>, to be the program's first director. Shriver fleshed out the organization with the help of Warren Wiggins and others.<sup id="cite_ref-m.rockymountainnews.com_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m.rockymountainnews.com-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Shriver and his <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tank</a> outlined the organization's goals and set the initial number of volunteers. The Peace Corps began recruiting in July 1962; <a href="/wiki/Bob_Hope" title="Bob Hope">Bob Hope</a> cut radio and television announcements hailing the program.
</p><p>A leading Peace Corps critic was <a href="/wiki/U.S._Representative" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Representative">U.S. Representative</a> <a href="/wiki/Otto_Passman" title="Otto Passman">Otto Passman</a> of <a href="/wiki/Louisiana%27s_5th_congressional_district" title="Louisiana&#39;s 5th congressional district">Louisiana's 5th congressional district</a>, based about <a href="/wiki/Monroe,_Louisiana" title="Monroe, Louisiana">Monroe</a>. Critics called Passman "Otto the Terrible" for trying to thwart the program by reducing its funding to minimal levels. Ultimately, it would be President Nixon, who despite his previous skepticism rescued the Peace Corps after 1969 from Passman's congressional knife.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Until about 1967, applicants had to pass a placement test of "general aptitude" (knowledge of various skills needed for Peace Corps assignments) and language aptitude.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> After an address from Kennedy, who was introduced by Rev. Russell Fuller of Memorial Christian Church, <a href="/wiki/Disciples_of_Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Disciples of Christ">Disciples of Christ</a>, on August 28, 1961, the first group of volunteers left for <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>, known as Tanganyika at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The program was formally authorized by <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Congress of the United States">Congress</a> on September 22, 1961, and within two years over 7,300 volunteers were serving in 44 countries. This number increased to 15,000 in June 1966, the largest number in the organization's history.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The organization experienced controversy in its first year of operation. On October 13, 1961, a <a href="/wiki/Postcard" title="Postcard">postcard</a> from a volunteer named Margery Jane Michelmore in <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> to a friend in the U.S. described her situation in Nigeria as "squalor and absolutely primitive living conditions."<sup id="cite_ref-squalor_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-squalor-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-infamous_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infamous-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> However, this postcard never made it out of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-infamous_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infamous-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Ibadan" title="University of Ibadan">University of Ibadan</a> College Students Union demanded deportation and accused the volunteers of being "America's international <a href="/wiki/Espionage" title="Espionage">spies</a>" and the project as "a scheme designed to foster <a href="/wiki/Neocolonialism" title="Neocolonialism">neocolonialism</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-postcard_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-postcard-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Soon the international press picked up the story, leading several people in the U.S. administration to question the program.<sup id="cite_ref-healing_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-healing-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Nigerian students protested the program, while the American volunteers sequestered themselves and eventually began a <a href="/wiki/Hunger_strike" title="Hunger strike">hunger strike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-infamous_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-infamous-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> After several days, the Nigerian students agreed to open a dialogue with the Americans.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Policies">Policies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Policies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table role="presentation" class="mbox-small noprint" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">
<tbody><tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><div class="center"><div class="floatnone"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="50" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="160" data-file-height="160" /></div></div></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em"><div class="haudio">
<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg" title="File:JFK Establishment of the Peace Corps edit.ogg">Establishment of the Peace Corps</a></div>
<div><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_1" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="82.337959183673" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/56/JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (71 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="71292" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/56/JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg/JFK_Establishment_of_the_Peace_Corps_edit.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="135424" /></audio></div></div>
<div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">John F. Kennedy's announcement of the establishment of the Peace Corps</div></div></td></tr>
<tr><td colspan="2" class="mbox-text" style="line-height:1.1em"><hr /><i class="selfreference">Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Help:Media" title="Help:Media">media help</a>.</i></td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<p>The theme of enabling Americans to volunteer in poor countries appealed to Kennedy because it fit in with his campaign themes of self-sacrifice and volunteerism, while also providing a way to redefine American relations with the Third World. Upon taking office, Kennedy issued an executive order establishing the Peace Corps, and he named his brother-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Sargent_Shriver" title="Sargent Shriver">Sargent Shriver</a>, as the agency's first director. Shriver, not Kennedy, energetically lobbied Congress for approval. Kennedy proudly took the credit, and ensured that it remained free of CIA influence. He largely left its administration to Shriver. To avoid the appearance of favoritism to the Catholic Church, the Corps did not place its volunteers with any religious agencies.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In the first twenty-five years, more than 100,000 Americans served in 44 countries as part of the program. Most volunteers taught English in local schools, but many became involved in activities like construction and food delivery.  Shriver practiced affirmative action, and women comprised about 40 percent of the first 7000 volunteers. However given the paucity of black college graduates, racial minorities never reached five percent. The Corps developed its own training program, based on nine weeks at an American university, with a focus on conversational language, world affairs, and desired job skills.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> That was followed by three weeks at a Peace Corps camp in Puerto Rico, and week or two of orientation the home and the host country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBernstein1991259-79_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBernstein1991259-79-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="1970.E2.80.931999"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1970–1999">1970–1999</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: 1970–1999">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In July 1971, President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a>, an opponent of the program,<sup id="cite_ref-founding_docs_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-founding_docs-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gibson_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gibson-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tobin_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tobin-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> brought the Peace Corps under the umbrella agency <a href="/wiki/ACTION_(U.S._government_agency)" title="ACTION (U.S. government agency)">ACTION</a>. President <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>, an advocate of the program, said that his mother, who had served as a nurse in the program, had "one of the most glorious experiences of her life" in the Peace Corps.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In 1979, he made it fully autonomous in an executive order. This independent status was further secured by 1981 legislation making the organization an independent federal agency.
</p><p>In 1976, <a href="/wiki/Murder_of_Deborah_Gardner" title="Murder of Deborah Gardner">Deborah Gardner</a> was found murdered in her home in Tonga, where she was serving in the Peace Corps. Dennis Priven, a fellow Peace Corps worker, was later charged with the murder by the Tonga government.<sup id="cite_ref-Murder_in_Peacecorps_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Murder_in_Peacecorps-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> He was found not guilty by reason of insanity, and was sentenced to serve time in a mental institution in Washington D.C. Priven was never admitted to any institution, and the handling of the case has been heavily criticized. The main criticism has been that the Peace Corps seemingly worked to keep one of its volunteers from being found guilty of murder, due to the reflection it would have on the organization.<sup id="cite_ref-Deborah_Gardner&#39;s_Death_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deborah_Gardner&#39;s_Death-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="2000.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2000–present">2000–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: 2000–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although the earliest volunteers were typically thought of as generalists, the Peace Corps had requests for technical personnel from the start.  For example, geologists were among the first volunteers requested by <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a>, an early volunteer host.  An article in <i>Geotimes</i> (a trade publication) in 1963, reviewed the program, with a follow-up history of Peace Corps geoscientists appearing in that publication in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>  During the Nixon Administration the Peace Corps included foresters, <a href="/wiki/Computer_scientist" title="Computer scientist">computer scientists</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Small_business" title="Small business">small business</a> advisers among its volunteers.
</p><p>In 1982, President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> appointed director <a href="/wiki/Loret_Miller_Ruppe" title="Loret Miller Ruppe">Loret Miller Ruppe</a>, who initiated business-related programs. For the first time, a significant number of conservative and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Republican_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Republican Party">Republican</a> volunteers joined the Corps, as the organization continued to reflect the evolving political and social conditions in the United States.  Funding cuts during the early 1980s reduced the number of volunteers to 5,380, its lowest level since the early years. Funding increased in 1985, when Congress began raising the number of volunteers, reaching 10,000 in 1992.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Madagascar_Peace_Corps_volunteers_swearing_in,_April_2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Madagascar_Peace_Corps_volunteers_swearing_in%2C_April_2006.jpg/220px-Madagascar_Peace_Corps_volunteers_swearing_in%2C_April_2006.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Madagascar_Peace_Corps_volunteers_swearing_in%2C_April_2006.jpg/330px-Madagascar_Peace_Corps_volunteers_swearing_in%2C_April_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Madagascar_Peace_Corps_volunteers_swearing_in%2C_April_2006.jpg/440px-Madagascar_Peace_Corps_volunteers_swearing_in%2C_April_2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="778" data-file-height="518" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Madagascar_Peace_Corps_volunteers_swearing_in,_April_2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Peace Corps trainees swearing in as volunteers in <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, April 26, 2006.</div></div></div>
<p>After the 2001 <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a>, which alerted the U.S. to growing <a href="/wiki/Anti-Americanism" title="Anti-Americanism">anti-U.S. sentiment</a> in the Middle East, President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> pledged to double the size of the organization within five years as a part of the <a href="/wiki/War_on_Terrorism" class="mw-redirect" title="War on Terrorism">War on Terrorism</a>. For the 2004 fiscal year, Congress increased the budget to US$325&#160;million, US$30&#160;million above that of 2003 but US$30&#160;million below the President's request.
</p><p>As part of an <a href="/wiki/Economic_stimulus" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic stimulus">economic stimulus</a> package in 2008, President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> proposed to double the size of the Peace Corps.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> However, as of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the amount requested was insufficient to reach this goal by 2011. In fact, the number of applicants to the Peace Corps declined steadily from a high of 15,384 in 2009 to 10,118 in 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Congress raised the 2010 appropriation from the US$373&#160;million requested by the President to US$400&#160;million, and proposed bills would raise this further for 2011 and 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> According to former director <a href="/wiki/Gaddi_Vasquez" title="Gaddi Vasquez">Gaddi Vasquez</a>, the Peace Corps is trying to recruit more diverse volunteers of different ages and make it look "more like America".<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> A <i>Harvard International Review</i> article from 2007 proposed to expand the Peace Corps, revisit its mission, and equip it with new technology.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In 1961 only 1% of volunteers were over 50, compared with 5% today. Ethnic minorities currently comprise 19% of volunteers.<sup id="cite_ref-fast_facts_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fast_facts-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> 35% of the U.S. population are Hispanic or non-White.<sup id="cite_ref-s0201_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-s0201-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009, Casey Frazee, who was sexually assaulted while serving in South Africa, created First Response Action, an <a href="/wiki/Advocacy_group" title="Advocacy group">advocacy group</a> for a stronger Peace Corps response for volunteers who are survivors or victims of physical and sexual violence.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, concerns about the safety of volunteers were illustrated by a report, compiled from official public documents, listing hundreds of violent crimes against volunteers since 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, a <i><a href="/wiki/20/20_(US_television_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="20/20 (US television series)">20/20</a></i> investigation found that "more than 1,000 young American women have been raped or sexually assaulted in the last decade while serving as Peace Corps volunteers in foreign countries."<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_presence">International presence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: International presence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>During its history, Peace Corps Volunteers have worked in the following countries:<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Latin America and the Caribbean</b> (23% of volunteers serve here, 2019)

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Application_and_Volunteer_Process">Application and Volunteer Process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Application and Volunteer Process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The application for the Peace Corps takes up to one hour, unless one talks to a recruiter. The applicant must be at least 18 years old and a U.S. citizen and, according to a 2018 document, they should apply 6 to 9 months before they want to leave. They must go through an interview.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Applicants can apply to only one placement every year. Placements can be sorted through the Peace Corps six project sectors: Agriculture, Environment, Community Economic Development, Health, Education, and Youth in Development. Applicants may also narrow down their application of choice by country they want to serve in various regions of the world. 
</p><p>Peace Corps volunteers are expected to serve for 2 years in the foreign country, with 3 months of training experience before their departure to their service country.
</p><p>Prior to 2014, the application process took about a year.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Initiatives">Initiatives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Initiatives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Peace Corps aims to educate community members on the different illnesses that are present in developing countries as well as what treatments exist in order prevent these illnesses from spreading. Volunteers are also often there in order to teach community members about modern agricultural techniques in order for them to more effectively produce food for themselves and each other (Peace Corps). The Corps is also a proponent of equal education and moves to allow for equal education opportunities for girls in countries like Liberia and Ethiopia. In 2015, the organization partnered with <a href="/wiki/United_States_Agency_for_International_Development" title="United States Agency for International Development">United States Agency for International Development</a> (USAID) to implement First Lady <a href="/wiki/Michelle_Obama" title="Michelle Obama">Michelle Obama</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Let_Girls_Learn" class="mw-redirect" title="Let Girls Learn">Let Girls Learn</a> initiative.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eradicating_malaria_in_Africa">Eradicating malaria in Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Eradicating malaria in Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Corps launched its initiative to engage volunteers in <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> control efforts in 2011. The initiative, which grew out of malaria prevention programs in Peace Corps Senegal, now includes volunteers in 24 African countries.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environment">Environment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Environment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Corps offers a variety of environmental programs. Needs assessments determine which programs apply to each country. Programs include effective and efficient forms of farming, recycling, park management, environmental education, and developing <a href="/wiki/Alternative_fuel" title="Alternative fuel">alternative fuel</a> sources.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>  Volunteers must have some combination of academic degrees and practical experience.
</p><p>The three major programs are Protected-Areas Management, Environment Education or Awareness, and Forestry.
</p><p>In <i>Protected areas management</i>, volunteers work with parks or other programs to teach resource conservation. Volunteer activities include technical training, working with park staff on wildlife preservation, organizing community-based conservation programs for sustainable use of forests or marine resources, and creating activities for raising revenue to protect the environment.
</p><p><i>Environment Education or Awareness</i> focuses on communities that have environmental issues regarding farming and income. Programs include teaching in elementary and secondary schools; environmental education to youth programs; creation of environmental groups; support forest and marine resource sustainability; ways of generating money; urban sanitation management; and educating farmers about soil conservation, forestry, and vegetable gardening.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Forestry</i> programs help communities conserve natural resources through projects such as soil conservation, flood control, creation of sustainable fuels, agroforestry (e.g., fruit and vegetable production), alley cropping, and protection of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace_Corps_Response">Peace Corps Response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Peace Corps Response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Peace Corps Response, formerly named the Crisis Corps, was created by Peace Corps Director Mark Gearan in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-PCOLGearancrisiscorps_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCOLGearancrisiscorps-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Gearan modeled the Crisis Corps after the <a href="/wiki/National_Peace_Corps_Association" title="National Peace Corps Association">National Peace Corps Association</a>'s successful Emergency Response Network (ERN) of Returned Peace Corps Volunteers willing to respond to crises when needed. ERN emerged in response to the 1994 <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_genocide" title="Rwandan genocide">Rwandan genocide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> On November 19, 2007 Peace Corps Director Ronald Tschetter changed Crisis Corps's name to Peace Corps Response.<sup id="cite_ref-peacecorpsresponsecrisiscorps_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peacecorpsresponsecrisiscorps-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The change to Peace Corps Response allowed Peace Corps to include projects that did not rise to the level of a crisis. The program deploys former volunteers on high-impact assignments that typically range from three to twelve months in duration.
</p><p>Peace Corps Response volunteers generally receive the same allowances and benefits as their Peace Corps counterparts, including round-trip transportation, living and readjustment allowances, and medical care. Minimum qualifications include completion of at least one year of Peace Corps service, including training, in addition to medical and legal clearances. The Crisis Corps title was retained as a unique branch within Peace Corps Response, designed for volunteers who are deployed to true "crisis" situations, such as disaster relief following hurricanes, earthquakes, floods, volcanic eruptions and other catastrophes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_languages">Education and languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Education and languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Peace Corps has created resources for teachers in the US and abroad to teach 101 languages.<sup id="cite_ref-livel_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livel-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-yojik_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yojik-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Resources vary by language, and include text, recordings, lesson plans and teaching notes.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws_governing_the_Peace_Corps">Laws governing the Peace Corps</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Laws governing the Peace Corps">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Executive_orders">Executive orders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Executive orders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Peace Corps was originally established by Executive Order, and has been modified by several subsequent executive orders including:
</p>
<ul><li>1961 – <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Executive_Order_10924" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Executive Order 10924">Executive Order 10924</a> – Establishment and administration of the Peace Corps in the Department of State (Kennedy)<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1962 – <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Executive_Order_11041" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Executive Order 11041">Executive Order 11041</a> – Continuance and administration of the Peace Corps in the Department of State (Kennedy)<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1963 – <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Executive_Order_11103" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Executive Order 11103">Executive Order 11103</a> – Providing for the appointment of former Peace Corps volunteers to the civilian career services (Kennedy)<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1971 – <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Executive_Order_11603" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Executive Order 11603">Executive Order 11603</a> – Assigning additional functions to the Director of ACTION (Nixon)</li>
<li>1979 – <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Executive_Order_12137" class="extiw" title="wikisource:Executive Order 12137">Executive Order 12137</a> – The Peace Corps (Carter)<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws">Laws</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Laws">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Federal laws governing the Peace Corps are contained in <a href="/wiki/Title_22_of_the_United_States_Code" title="Title 22 of the United States Code">Title 22 of the United States Code</a> – Foreign Relations and Intercourse, Chapter 34 – The Peace Corps.<sup id="cite_ref-uscode_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uscode-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Public laws are passed by Congress and the President and create or modify the U.S. Code.  The first public law establishing Peace Corps in the US Code was The Peace Corps Act passed by the 87th Congress and signed into law on September 22, 1961.  Several public laws have modified the Peace Corps Act, including:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-87-293">87–293</a>, 75&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-75-612">612</a>, enacted September&#160;22, 1961 – The Peace Corps Act</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-88-200">88–200</a>, 77&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-77-359">359</a>, enacted December&#160;13, 1963</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-89-134">89–134</a>, 79&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-79-549">549</a>, enacted August&#160;24, 1965</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-89-554">89–554</a>, 80&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-80-378">378</a>, enacted September&#160;6, 1966</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-89-572">89–572</a>, 80&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-80-764">764</a>, enacted September&#160;13, 1966</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-91-99">91–99</a>, 83&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-83-166">166</a>, enacted October&#160;29, 1969</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-91-352">91–352</a>, 84&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-84-464">464</a>, enacted July&#160;24, 1970</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-94-130">94–130</a>, 89&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-89-684">684</a>, enacted November&#160;14, 1975 – Bill to carry into effect certain provisions of the Patent Cooperation Treaty, and for other purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-95-331">95–331</a>, 92&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-92-414">414</a>, enacted August&#160;2, 1978 – Peace Corps Act Amendments<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-96-465">96–465</a>, 94&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-94-2071">2071</a>, enacted October&#160;17, 1980 – The Foreign Service Act of 1980<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-97-113">97–113</a>, 95&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-95-1519">1519</a>, enacted December&#160;29, 1981 – International Security and Development Cooperation Act of 1981<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-99-83">99–83</a>, 99&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-99-190">190</a>, enacted August&#160;8, 1985 – International Security and Development Cooperation Act of 1985<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-99-514">99–514</a>, 100&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-100-2085">2085</a>, enacted October&#160;22, 1986 – Tax Reform Act of 1986<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-102-565">102–565</a>, 106&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-106-4265">4265</a>, enacted October&#160;28, 1992 – A bill to amend the Peace Corps Act to authorize appropriations for the Peace Corps for FY1993 and to establish Peace Corps foreign exchange fluctuations account, and for other purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-105-12">105–12</a>, 111&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-111-23">23</a>, enacted April&#160;30, 1997 – The Assisted Suicide Funding Restriction Act of 1997<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Act_of_Congress" title="Act of Congress">Pub.L.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/pl-106-30">106–30</a>, 113&#160;<a href="/wiki/United_States_Statutes_at_Large" title="United States Statutes at Large">Stat.</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://legislink.org/us/stat-113-55">55</a>, enacted May&#160;21, 1999 – Peace Corps Act, FY2002, 2003 Authorization Bill<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.congress.gov/bill/110th-congress/house-bill/5535">Peace Corps Reauthorization Act of 2008</a>&#32;at <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress.gov</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Code_of_Federal_Regulations">Code of Federal Regulations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Code of Federal Regulations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Peace Corps is subject to Federal Regulations as prescribed by public law and executive order and contained in <a href="/wiki/Title_22_of_the_Code_of_Federal_Regulations" title="Title 22 of the Code of Federal Regulations">Title 22 of the Code of Federal Regulations</a> under Chapter 3.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Limitations_on_former_volunteers">Limitations on former volunteers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Limitations on former volunteers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Former members of the Peace Corps may not be assigned to <a href="/wiki/Military_intelligence" title="Military intelligence">military intelligence</a> duties for a period of 4 years following Peace Corps service. Furthermore, they are forever prohibited from serving in a military intelligence posting to any country in which they volunteered.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time_limits_on_employment">Time limits on employment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Time limits on employment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Peace Corps employees receive time-limited appointments, and most employees are limited to a maximum of five years of employment. This time limit was established to ensure that Peace Corps' staff remain fresh and innovative. A related rule specifies that former employees cannot be re-employed until after the same amount of time that they were employed. Volunteer service is not counted for the purposes of either rule.<sup id="cite_ref-fiveyearrule_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fiveyearrule-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Union_representation">Union representation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Union representation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2018</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Non-supervisory domestic employees are represented by the <a href="/wiki/American_Federation_of_State,_County,_and_Municipal_Employees" class="mw-redirect" title="American Federation of State, County, and Municipal Employees">American Federation of State, County, and Municipal Employees</a> (AFSCME) Local 3548.  The Federal Labor Relations Agency certified the Union on May 11, 1983.  About 500 domestic employees are members. The current <a href="/wiki/Collective_bargaining" title="Collective bargaining">collective bargaining</a> agreement became effective on April 21, 1995.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership">Leadership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Leadership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Directors">Directors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Directors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On January 3, 2018, President <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a> nominated Josephine "Jody" Olsen as the 20th director of the Peace Corps.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Olsen has a long history with the agency, serving as Acting Director in 2009, Deputy Director from 2002 to 2009, Chief of Staff from 1989 to 1992, Regional Director, North Africa Near East, Asia, Pacific from 1981 to 1984, and Country Director in Togo from 1979 to 1981. Olsen also served as a Peace Corps volunteer in Tunisia from 1966 to 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<td>O'Donnell's appointment was the first for a former Peace Corps country director (Korea, 1966–70). He fought budget cuts, and believed strongly in a non-career Peace Corps.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>5</td>
<td>Donald Hess</td>
<td>1972–1973</td>
<td>Nixon</td>
<td>Hess initiated training of volunteers in the host country where they would eventually serve, using host country nationals. The training provided more realistic preparation, and costs dropped for the agency. Hess also sought to end the down-sizing of the Peace Corps.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>6</td>
<td>Nicholas Craw</td>
<td>1973–1974</td>
<td>Nixon</td>
<td>Craw sought to increase the number of volunteers in the field and to stabilize the agency's future. He introduced a goal-setting measurement plan, the Country Management Plan, which gained increased Congressional support and improved resource allocation across the 69 participating countries.
</td></tr>

<td>1975–1977</td>
<td>Ford</td>
<td>Dellenback improved volunteer health care available. He emphasized recruiting generalists. He believed in committed applicants even those without specific skills and instead training them for service.
</td></tr>

<td>Payton was the first female director and the first African American. She focused on improving volunteer diversity.
</td></tr>
<tr>

<td>1979–1981</td>
<td>Carter</td>
<td>Celeste focused on the role of women in development and increased women and minority participation, particularly for staff positions. He invested heavily in training, including the development of a worldwide core curriculum.
</td></tr>

<td>Ruppe was the longest-serving director and championed women in development roles. She launched the Competitive Enterprise Development program, the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean_Basin_Initiative" title="Caribbean Basin Initiative">Caribbean Basin Initiative</a>, the Initiative for Central America and the African Food Systems Initiative.
</td></tr>

<td>1989–1991</td>
<td>G.H.W. Bush</td>
<td>Coverdell established two programs with a domestic focus. World Wise Schools enabled U.S. students to correspond with overseas volunteers. Fellows/USA assisted Returned Peace Corps volunteers in pursuing graduate studies while serving local communities.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>12</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Elaine_Chao" title="Elaine Chao">Elaine Chao</a></td>
<td>1991–1992</td>
<td>G.H.W. Bush</td>
<td>Chao was the first Asian American director. She expanded Peace Corps' presence in Eastern Europe and Central Asia by establishing the first Peace Corps programs in <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a>, Lithuania, <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>, and other newly independent countries.

<td>1993–1995</td>
<td>Clinton</td>
<td>Bellamy was the first RPCV (Returned Peace Corps volunteer) (Guatemala 1963–65) to be director.  She reinvigorated relations with former volunteers and launched the Corps' web site.
</td></tr>
<tr>

<td>1995–1999</td>
<td>Clinton</td>
<td>Gearan established the <a href="/wiki/Crisis_Corps" class="mw-redirect" title="Crisis Corps">Crisis Corps</a>, a program that allows former volunteers to help overseas communities recover from natural disasters and humanitarian crises. He supported expanding the corps and opened <i>new volunteer programs</i> in South Africa, Jordan, <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>.

<td>1999–2001</td>
<td>Clinton</td>
<td>Schneider was the second RPCV (El Salvador, 1966–68) to head the agency. He launched an initiative to increase volunteers' participation in helping prevent the spread of HIV/AIDS in Africa, and also sought volunteers to work on information technology projects.
</td></tr>

<td>16</td>

</td></tr>

<td>September 2006 – 2008</td>
<td>G.W. Bush</td>
<td>The third RPCV to head the agency, Tschetter served in India in the mid-1960s. He launched an initiative known as the "50 and Over," to increase the participation of older men and women.

Williams cited personal and family considerations as the reason for his stepping down as Peace Corps Director on September 17, 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>

<td>Carrie Hessler-Radelet became acting Director of the Peace Corps in September 2012. Previously, Hessler-Radelet served as deputy director of the Peace Corps from June 23, 2010, until her appointment as acting Director.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> From 1981–83, she served as a Peace Corps volunteer in Western Samoa with her husband, Steve. She was confirmed as Director on June 5, 2014.

Olsen previously served the Peace Corps as Acting Director in 2009, Deputy Director from 2002 to 2009, Chief of Staff from 1989 to 1992, Regional Director, North Africa Near East, Asia, Pacific from 1981 to 1984, and Country Director in Togo from 1979 to 1981. Olsen also served as a Peace Corps volunteer in Tunisia from 1966 to 1968.
