<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inspector_General">Inspector General</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Inspector General">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Peace Corps Office of <a href="/wiki/Inspector_General#United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Inspector General">Inspector General</a> is authorized by law to review all programs and operations of the Peace Corps.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The OIG is an independent entity within the Peace Corps. The inspector general (IG) reports directly to the Peace Corps Director. In addition, the IG reports to Congress semiannually with data on OIG activities.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The OIG serves as the law enforcement arm of the Peace Corps and works closely with the Department of State, the Department of Justice, and other federal agencies OIG has three sections to conduct its functions:
</p>
     <blockquote><p>Audit – Auditors review functional activities of the Peace Corps,  such as contract compliance and financial and program operations, to ensure accountability and to recommend improved levels of economy and efficiency;
</p><p>     Evaluations – Evaluators analyze the management and program operations of the Peace Corps at both overseas posts and domestic offices. They identify best practices and recommend program improvements and ways to accomplish Peace Corps' mission and strategic goals.
</p><p>     Investigations – Investigators respond to allegations of criminal or administrative wrongdoing by Peace Corps Volunteers, Peace Corps personnel, including experts and consultants, and by those who do business with the Peace Corps, including contractors.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>From 2006–07, <a href="/wiki/H._David_Kotz" title="H. David Kotz">H. David Kotz</a> was the Inspector General.<sup id="cite_ref-SEC01_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SEC01-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Criticism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Critics and criticisms of Peace Corps include Robert L. Strauss of <i><a href="/wiki/Foreign_Policy" title="Foreign Policy">Foreign Policy</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> an article by a former volunteer describing assaults on volunteers from 1992 to 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> an ABC news report on <i><a href="/wiki/20/20_(U.S._TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="20/20 (U.S. TV series)">20/20</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> a <i><a href="/wiki/Huffington_Post" class="mw-redirect" title="Huffington Post">Huffington Post</a></i> article on former Peace Corps volunteers speaking out on rapes,<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> and About.com's article on rape and assault in the Peace Corps.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Reagan_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Reagan Administration">Reagan Administration</a>, in 1986, an article in the <a href="/wiki/Multinational_Monitor" title="Multinational Monitor">Multinational Monitor</a> looked critically at the Peace Corps.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> On a positive note, the writer praises the Corps for aspects saying that it is "not in the business of transferring massive economic resources. Rather it concentrates on increasing productivity and encouraging self-reliance in villages that are often ignored by large-scale development agencies," and notes the "heavy emphasis on basic education" by the Corps.
"Many returned volunteers complain that the Peace Corps does little to promote or make use of their rich experiences once they return ... [A] Peace Corps volunteer is sent in ... [to] relieve ... the local government from having to develop policies that assure equitable distribution of health care ... During the early years there were many failures in structure and programming ... Some critics charge that the Peace Corps is only a somewhat ineffective attempt to counter damage done to the U.S. image abroad by its aggressive military and its unscrupulous businesses ... Many observers and some returned volunteers charge that, in addition to public relations for the United States, Peace Corps programs serve to legitimize dictators ... When he began evaluating the Corps in the 1960s, Charlie Peters found "they were training volunteers to be junior diplomats. Giving them a course in American studies, world affairs and communism ... Although it seems unlikely that the Peace Corps is used in covert operations, wittingly or not it is often used in conjunction with U.S. military interests ... In a review of the Peace Corps in March the House Select Committee on Hunger praised the agency for effective work in the areas of agriculture and conservation, while recommending that the Corps expand its African Food Systems Initiative, increase the number of volunteers in the field, recruit more women, and move to depoliticize country dictatorships."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The author suggests that "the poor should be encouraged to organize a power base to gain more leverage with the powers-that-be" by the Peace Corps and that "The Peace Corps is the epitome of Kennedy's Camelot mythology. It is a tall order to expect a small program appended to an immense superpower, to make a difference, but it is a goal worth striving for."
</p><p>In December 2003, a report by the <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a> praised the Peace Corps but proposed changes.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> These include relabeling Peace Corps volunteers in certain countries, greater host country ownership, reverse volunteers (have volunteers from the host country in the U.S.), and multilateral volunteers. The Brookings Institution wrote that a "one-year service commitment [for the Baby Boom generation] could make the Peace Corps more attractive to older Americans, possibly combined with the option of returning to the same site or country after a three-month break" and customized placement to a specific country would increase the number of people volunteering.
</p><p>In a critique by The Future of Freedom Foundation,<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/James_Bovard" title="James Bovard">James Bovard</a> mixes history of the Peace Corps with current interpretations. He writes that in the 1980s, "The Peace Corps's world-saving pretensions were a joke on American taxpayers and Third World folks who expected real help." He goes on to criticize the difference in rhetoric and action of Peace Corps volunteers, even attacking its establishment as "the epitome of emotionalism in American politics." Using snippets of reports, accounts of those in countries affected by the Peace Corps and even concluded that at one point "some Peace Corps agricultural efforts directly hurt Third World poor."  At the end of the article, Bovard noted that all Peace Corps volunteers he had talked with conceded they have not helped foreigners ... but he acknowledges that "Some Peace Corps volunteers, like some Americans who volunteer for religion missions abroad, have truly helped foreigners."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_assault">Sexual assault</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Sexual assault">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Peace Corps has been criticized for failing to properly respond to the <a href="/wiki/Sexual_violence" title="Sexual violence">sexual violence</a> that many of its female volunteers face.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> BoingBoing editor Xeni Jardin describes criticism of the agency's response to assault: "A growing number of ex-Peace Corps volunteers are speaking out about having survived rape and other forms of sexual assault while assigned overseas. They say the agency ignored their concerns for safety or requests for relocation, and tried to blame rape victims for their attacks. Their stories, and support from families and advocates, are drawing attention from lawmakers and promises of reform from the agency". Among 8,655 volunteers there are on average 22 Peace Corps women who reported being the victims of rape or attempted rape each year.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At a meeting of the House Foreign Affairs Committee in 2011, Peace Corps volunteers shared their experiences of violence and sexual assault. At this meeting, it was found that between 2000–2009 there have been several cases of rape or attempted rape, and about 22 women  are sexually assaulted each year. The case of murdered Peace Corps volunteer Kate Puzey was discussed. The Peace Corps has gained attention in the media and their directors have been attacked for how they handled this situation. Kate Puzey's mother was one of those to make a comment at the meeting about how badly the situation with her daughter had been handled. One woman claimed that her country's director had blamed her for getting raped, while other victims have also been similarly blamed.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Criticism of how Peace Corps has responded to sexual assaults against volunteers culminated in the appointment of Kellie Green as the agency's first Director of the Office Of Victims Advocacy in 2011. Green was eventually pushed out of her position in April 2015 for purportedly "creating a hostile work environment". Greene maintains that Peace Corps retaliated against her for pressing agency officials to fully comply with their responsibilities towards volunteers who have been victims of sexual assault. A Change.org petition demanding that Green be reinstated began circulating among former volunteers in December 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2009, the most recent year reported, 69% of Peace Corps crime victims were women, 88% were under 30, and 82% were Caucasian. Worldwide, there were 15 cases of rape/attempted rape and 96 cases of sexual assault reported for a total of 111 sexual crimes committed against female Peace Corps volunteers. The majority of women who join the Peace Corps are in their mid-twenties. In 62% of the more than 2,900 assault cases since 1990, the victim was identified as being alone. In 59% of assault cases, the victim was identified as a woman in her 20s.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Abortion is also an issue for female Peace Corps volunteers. Women who have been raped in foreign countries while volunteering have wanted to get abortions. The low pay of Peace Corp volunteers combined with the expense of an abortion often makes obtaining one difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Since 1979, it has been prohibited for federal funds to cover abortion costs for Peace Corps volunteers. Even in instances of life endangerment, rape and incest, volunteers are not financially covered. Volunteers do receive other forms of health care, including prescription medications, routine examinations, and emergency care, free of charge. These services are provided in-country through a Peace Corps Medical Officer. The abortion topic is very active in the Peace Corps, because when volunteers become pregnant for any reason, they can no longer be volunteers, and a significant number of them want to remain volunteers.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Peace_Corps&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Frank_Zappa" title="Frank Zappa">Frank Zappa</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Mothers_of_Invention" title="The Mothers of Invention">The Mothers of Invention</a> have a song named "<a href="/wiki/Who_Needs_the_Peace_Corps%3F" title="Who Needs the Peace Corps?">Who Needs the Peace Corps?</a>" on their 1968 album <i><a href="/wiki/We%27re_Only_in_It_for_the_Money" title="We&#39;re Only in It for the Money">We're Only in It for the Money</a></i>.
</p><p>In popular culture, the Peace Corps has been used as a comedic <a href="/wiki/Plot_device" title="Plot device">plot device</a> in such movies as <i><a href="/wiki/Airplane!" title="Airplane!">Airplane!</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Christmas_with_the_Kranks" title="Christmas with the Kranks">Christmas with the Kranks</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Shallow_Hal" title="Shallow Hal">Shallow Hal</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Volunteers_(1985_film)" title="Volunteers (1985 film)">Volunteers</a></i> or used to set the scene for a historic era, as when Frances "Baby" Houseman tells the audience she plans to join the Peace Corps in the introduction to the movie <i><a href="/wiki/Dirty_Dancing" title="Dirty Dancing">Dirty Dancing</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Peace Corps has also been documented on film and examined more seriously and in more depth. The 2006 documentary film <i>Death of Two Sons,</i> directed by Micah Schaffer, juxtaposes the deaths of <a href="/wiki/Amadou_Diallo" class="mw-redirect" title="Amadou Diallo">Amadou Diallo</a>, a Guinean-American who was gunned down by four New York City policemen with 41 bullets, and Peace Corps volunteer Jesse Thyne who lived with Amadou's family in Guinea and died in a car crash there.<sup id="cite_ref-PCOLDiallo_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCOLDiallo-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> <i>Jimi Sir</i>, released in 2007, is a documentary portrait of volunteer James Parks' experiences as a high school science, math and English teacher during the last 10 weeks of his service in <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PCOLSirjimi_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCOLSirjimi-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> James speaks Nepali fluently and shows a culture where there are no roads, vehicles, electricity, plumbing, telephone or radio.<sup id="cite_ref-PCOLSirjimi_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCOLSirjimi-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The movie <i><a href="/wiki/El_Rey_(film)" title="El Rey (film)">El Rey</a></i>, directed and written by Antonio Dorado in 2004, attacks corrupt police, unscrupulous politicians and half-hearted revolutionaries but also depicts the <a href="/wiki/Urban_legend" title="Urban legend">urban legend</a> of Peace Corps Volunteers "training" native Colombians how to process coca leaves into cocaine.<sup id="cite_ref-PCOLElrey_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCOLElrey-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the 1969 film, <i><a href="/wiki/Yawar_Mallku" class="mw-redirect" title="Yawar Mallku">Yawar Mallku</a>/Sangre de cóndor/Blood of the Condor</i>, <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivian</a> director <a href="/wiki/Jorge_Sanjin%C3%A9s" title="Jorge Sanjinés">Jorge Sanjinés</a> portrayed Peace Corps volunteers in the camp as arrogant, ethnocentric, and narrow-minded imperialists out to destroy Indian culture. One particularly powerful scene showed Indians attacking a clinic while the volunteers inside sterilized Indian women against their will. The film is thought to be at least partially responsible for the expulsion of the Peace Corps from Bolivia in 1971. Peace Corps volunteer Fred Krieger who was serving in Bolivia at the time said, "It was an effective movie – emotionally very arousing – and it directly targeted Peace Corps volunteers. I thought I would be lynched before getting out of the theatre. To my amazement, people around me smiled courteously as we left, no one commented, it was just like any other movie."<sup id="cite_ref-PCOLLlama_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PCOLLlama-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2016, Peace Corps partnered with jewelry retailer <a href="/wiki/Alex_and_Ani" title="Alex and Ani">Alex and Ani</a> to create cord bracelets to raise money for the Peace Corps' Let Girls Learn Fund.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Fictional Peace Corps volunteers</b>
</p>
<ul><li>Frances "Baby" Houseman in the 1987 film <a href="/wiki/Dirty_Dancing" title="Dirty Dancing">Dirty Dancing</a> plans to join the Peace Corps after graduating from Mount Holyoke.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Boy_Meets_World" title="Boy Meets World">Boy Meets World</a>, Jack and Rachel graduate from the fictional Pennbrook University and join the Peace Corps.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
